<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Griffith Stadium</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>

<p><b>Griffith Stadium</b> was a sports <a href="/wiki/Stadium" title="Stadium">stadium</a> that stood in Washington, D.C., from 1911 to 1965, between <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Avenue" title="Georgia Avenue">Georgia Avenue</a> and 5th Street, and between W Street and Florida Avenue NW.</p>
<p>An earlier wooden <a href="/wiki/Baseball_park" title="Baseball park">baseball park</a> had been built on the same site in 1891. It was called <b><a href="/wiki/Boundary_Field" title="Boundary Field">Boundary Field</a></b>, or <b>National Park</b> as its occupants were then known primarily by the nickname Nationals. This park was destroyed by a fire in March 1911 and replaced by a steel and concrete structure, also at first called National Park and then American League Park; it was renamed for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Senators_(1901%E2%80%9360)" title="Washington Senators (1901–60)">Washington Senators</a> owner <a href="/wiki/Clark_Griffith" title="Clark Griffith">Clark Griffith</a> in 1923. The stadium was home to the <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> Senators from <a href="/wiki/1911_in_baseball" title="1911 in baseball">1911</a> through <a href="/wiki/1960_Washington_Senators_season" title="1960 Washington Senators season">1960</a>, and to an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Senators_(1961%E2%80%9371)" title="Washington Senators (1961–71)">expansion team of the same name</a> for their <a href="/wiki/1961_Washington_Senators_season" title="1961 Washington Senators season">first season</a> in <a href="/wiki/1961_in_baseball" title="1961 in baseball">1961</a>.</p>
<p>The venue hosted the <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="Major League Baseball All-Star Game">All-Star Game</a> in <a href="/wiki/1937_Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="1937 Major League Baseball All-Star Game">1937</a> and <a href="/wiki/1956_Major_League_Baseball_All-Star_Game" title="1956 Major League Baseball All-Star Game">1956</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/World_Series" title="World Series">World Series</a> games in <a href="/wiki/1924_World_Series" title="1924 World Series">1924</a>, <a href="/wiki/1925_World_Series" title="1925 World Series">1925</a>, and <a href="/wiki/1933_World_Series" title="1933 World Series">1933</a>. It served as home for the <a href="/wiki/Negro_league_baseball" title="Negro league baseball">Negro league</a> <a href="/wiki/Homestead_Grays" title="Homestead Grays">Homestead Grays</a> during the 1940s. It was also home to the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Redskins" title="Washington Redskins">Washington Redskins</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League" title="National Football League">National Football League</a> for 24 seasons, from the time they transferred from <a href="/wiki/1936_Boston_Redskins_season" title="1936 Boston Redskins season">Boston</a> in <a href="/wiki/1937_Washington_Redskins_season" title="1937 Washington Redskins season">1937</a> through the <a href="/wiki/1960_Washington_Redskins_season" title="1960 Washington Redskins season">1960</a> season.</p>
<p>The ballpark was demolished in 1965 and the <a href="/wiki/Howard_University_Hospital" title="Howard University Hospital">Howard University Hospital</a> now occupies the site.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Griffith_Stadium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>On March 17, 1911, Boundary Field, also known as National Park and American League Park (II), was destroyed by a fire started by a plumber's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blowtorch" title="Blowtorch">blowtorch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowry_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lowry-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Snyder_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder-2">[2]</a></sup> This left the owners of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Senators_(1901%E2%80%9360)" title="Washington Senators (1901–60)">Washington Senators</a> in a difficult situation, due to the fact that spring training had already begun and opening day was less than a month away. Thomas C. Noyes, president of the Senators, gained approval from the club's board of directors to build a new ballpark with a steel grandstand on the same site as Boundary Field. The quick construction of the ballpark was reported by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i>: "Day and night the chanting of the negro laborers has been heard in the vicinity, like Aladdin's palace, the structure rose as if by magic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Snyder_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder-2">[2]</a></sup> Opening Day 1911, the grandstand was sufficiently stable to host President <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Red_Sox" title="Boston Red Sox">Boston Red Sox</a>, as well as 16,000 fans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Construction of Griffith Stadium continued while the Senators were on the road, and was not completed until July 24, 1911.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BallparkTour.com_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-BallparkTour.com-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Field_design">Field design</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Griffith_Stadium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Field design">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:257px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Griffith_Stadium_Outfield.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1489" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Griffith_Stadium_Outfield.jpg/255px-Griffith_Stadium_Outfield.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Griffith_Stadium_Outfield.jpg/383px-Griffith_Stadium_Outfield.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Griffith_Stadium_Outfield.jpg/510px-Griffith_Stadium_Outfield.jpg 2x" width="255"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Griffith_Stadium_Outfield.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Right field seats, and row houses with wildcat bleachers, before the high fence was installed</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The stadium was laid out at an angle within its block in the Washington street grid. Thus it was over 400 feet (120 m) down the left field line (east) to the bleachers (though this distance was shortened in later years by the construction of an inner fence). The fence also took an unusual right-angled jut into right-center field where a large tree and several apartment buildings stood, due to the unwillingness of the owners of the tree and those nearby houses to sell to the Senators' owners during construction of the stadium.</p>
<p>The right-field fence angled away from the infield sharply, which, in addition to a 30-foot (9 m) fence (to block the view from surrounding buildings) about 8 feet (2.4 m) inside the lower, outer wall, meant that relatively few home runs were hit at the stadium. The field's orientation was unorthodox, as center field was east-southeast of home plate, which made for difficult visibility for the fielders in the late afternoon sun; recommended alignment is east-northeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Elevation" title="Elevation">elevation</a> of the natural grass playing field was approximately 100 feet (30 m) above <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">sea level</a>.</p>
<p>The Senators' groundskeepers maintained a downhill slope from home plate to first base, supposedly to help accelerate slow Washington batters. However, Griffith's groundskeepers were still adept at keeping a fine sod field that was "compared to that of the best golf courses." This was in sharp contrast to the unkempt field that can be seen in photographs of Griffith prior to 1923.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leavengood_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leavengood-6">[6]</a></sup> For many years, the right field grandstand wall served as the right-field foul line for the last 15 feet (4.6 m) before the foul pole, making it impossible to catch a ball there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowry_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lowry-1">[1]</a></sup> The 41-foot (12 m) tall, hand-operated <a href="/wiki/Scoreboard" title="Scoreboard">scoreboard</a> in right-center was in play, as was the <a href="/wiki/National_Bohemian" title="National Bohemian">National Bohemian</a> beer sign, shaped like a bottle, 56 feet (17 m) above the playing field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leventhal_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leventhal-7">[7]</a></sup> At one point in his youth, future <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commissioner_of_Baseball_(MLB)" title="Commissioner of Baseball (MLB)">MLB Commissioner</a> <a href="/wiki/Bowie_Kuhn" title="Bowie Kuhn">Bowie Kuhn</a> was the operator of the scoreboard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Snyder_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The outfield seats (in fair territory) in left and center field butted up against 5th Street. The football field ran along the third base line.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Griffith_Stadium_Grandstand.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1116" data-file-width="1490" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Griffith_Stadium_Grandstand.jpg/200px-Griffith_Stadium_Grandstand.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Griffith_Stadium_Grandstand.jpg/300px-Griffith_Stadium_Grandstand.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Griffith_Stadium_Grandstand.jpg/400px-Griffith_Stadium_Grandstand.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Griffith_Stadium_Grandstand.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The new left field grandstand, (in foul territory) with a higher roof than the original</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_and_changes">Expansion and changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Griffith_Stadium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Expansion and changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the early 1920s, a trend began of fans flocking in great numbers to the stadium to see baseball's rising stars from opposing teams; when the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Yankees" title="New York Yankees">New York Yankees</a> came to Washington, the chance to see <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a> brought large crowds to the ballpark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leavengood_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leavengood-6">[6]</a></sup> Clark Griffith took advantage of this trend by making small improvements in the stadium in 1920 and 1921. These improvements included changing the main entrance of the park from <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Avenue" title="Georgia Avenue">Georgia Avenue</a> to a refurbished one on <a href="/wiki/Florida_Avenue" title="Florida Avenue">Florida Ave.</a>, and spending $3,200 to build an office for himself beneath the grandstand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leavengood_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-Leavengood-6">[6]</a></sup> After various other small improvements, on August 21, 1923, Griffith announced plans to greatly expand and refurbish the ballpark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leavengood_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-Leavengood-6">[6]</a></sup> Following the trend of ballparks being named for their teams' owners, that August announcement included renaming the venue from American League Park to Griffith Stadium. The stadium, built in 1911, had been hastily constructed and provided minimal seating and other features when compared with other ballparks. The planned expansion was reported to cost $100,000, and expand the <a href="/wiki/Seating_capacity" title="Seating capacity">seating capacity</a> to "about 35,000", a number that wound up being 32,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The new seating came from the complete rebuilding of the left-field grandstand into a double-decked tier. The new upper section was wider than the old, resulting in a roofline that was considerably higher than the roof of the main grandstand, leaving a visible 15-foot gap between the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leavengood_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-Leavengood-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> At that point the wooden left-field bleachers were also replaced, by a large concrete deck that ran from the foul pole across left field and into center field. The first-base pavilion remained single-decked. The first attempt at a high wall was constructed across right field.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ty_Cobb_sliding2-edit1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1998" data-file-width="3012" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Ty_Cobb_sliding2-edit1.jpg/220px-Ty_Cobb_sliding2-edit1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Ty_Cobb_sliding2-edit1.jpg/330px-Ty_Cobb_sliding2-edit1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Ty_Cobb_sliding2-edit1.jpg/440px-Ty_Cobb_sliding2-edit1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ty_Cobb_sliding2-edit1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Concrete bleachers in background as <a href="/wiki/Ty_Cobb" title="Ty Cobb">Ty Cobb</a> slides into third base in 1924</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By the time of the 1925 World Series the right field pavilion had also been double-decked, and the angling right field wall and its scoreboard were reconstructed to align with the low right field wall, about 8 feet inside of it. This also resulted in the unique inward-pointing 90° angle in center field. Both versions of the tall fence had the effect of keeping the neighbors in the adjacent row houses from watching the games for free.</p>
<p>Lights were installed for the 1941 season. The Senators played their first home night game on May 28.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The right-field fence was originally covered in various billboard advertisements, but in later years was painted a solid dark green. A bullpen area was built in right center field behind a short fence, providing a new target for left-handed batters. In the mid-1950s, an inner fence was erected across left and center field, to reduce the home run distances by 10 to 20 feet (3 to 6 m). This inner fence stayed in place through the ballpark's final season, 1961.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="A_presidential_destination">A presidential destination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Griffith_Stadium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: A presidential destination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em">
<caption>External video</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1937_All-Star_managers.jpg"><img alt="1937 All-Star managers.jpg" data-file-height="823" data-file-width="1024" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/1937_All-Star_managers.jpg/250px-1937_All-Star_managers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/1937_All-Star_managers.jpg/375px-1937_All-Star_managers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/1937_All-Star_managers.jpg/500px-1937_All-Star_managers.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QZQ3k-nVrAc" rel="nofollow">Jimmie DeShong Motion Picture Film (1937) featuring Franklin Delano Roosevelt walking</a> taken at the 1937 All-Star Game, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Historical_and_Museum_Commission" title="Pennsylvania Historical and Museum Commission">Pennsylvania Historical and Museum Commission</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PHMC_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-PHMC-11">[11]</a></sup> (5:37) FDR at 0:38-0:54 and 5:25-5:37</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Every president of the United States from <a href="/wiki/William_Howard_Taft" title="William Howard Taft">William Howard Taft</a> to <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> threw a <a href="/wiki/Ceremonial_first_pitch" title="Ceremonial first pitch">ceremonial first pitch</a> at least once at Griffith. The Senators even constructed a special presidential box near the first-base dugout for the annual ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> was a good friend of Clark Griffith, and had attended games at the stadium since his days as <a href="/wiki/Assistant_Secretary_of_the_Navy" title="Assistant Secretary of the Navy">Assistant Secretary of the Navy</a> in the 1910s. When FDR returned to Washington in 1933 as president, Griffith visited the White House early every season to give Roosevelt season passes; he also constructed a special ramp at the ballpark that accommodated the president's special mobility needs after his bout with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polio" title="Polio">polio</a> in 1921.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Snyder_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder-2">[2]</a></sup> On Opening Day in 1941, Roosevelt stood up in the stadium's presidential box on the arm of a military aide, and threw out the first pitch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Snyder_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder-2">[2]</a></sup> In 1942 Clark Griffith urged Roosevelt to keep baseball going during the war. Griffith took credit after Roosevelt's initial "Green Light letter", which allowed baseball to continue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Snyder_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Snyder-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_sluggers">Notable sluggers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Griffith_Stadium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Notable sluggers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6006" data-file-width="7975" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg/250px-1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg/375px-1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg/500px-1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1937_all_stars_crop_FINAL2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 1937 All-Star game, played at Griffith Stadium, featured these seven American League players, from left to right: <a href="/wiki/Lou_Gehrig" title="Lou Gehrig">Lou Gehrig</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Cronin" title="Joe Cronin">Joe Cronin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bill_Dickey" title="Bill Dickey">Bill Dickey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_DiMaggio" title="Joe DiMaggio">Joe DiMaggio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Gehringer" title="Charlie Gehringer">Charlie Gehringer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jimmie_Foxx" title="Jimmie Foxx">Jimmie Foxx</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hank_Greenberg" title="Hank Greenberg">Hank Greenberg</a>. All seven would eventually be elected to the Hall of Fame.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The distant fences were no problem for sluggers like <a href="/wiki/Josh_Gibson" title="Josh Gibson">Josh Gibson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Mantle" title="Mickey Mantle">Mickey Mantle</a>, and the Senators' own youngster <a href="/wiki/Harmon_Killebrew" title="Harmon Killebrew">Harmon Killebrew</a>. There have been only three reported instances of a player hitting a home run <i>over</i> the left field bleachers: once by Mantle and twice by Gibson.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a> hit near-500-foot drives over the center and right-center walls on consecutive days in May 1921.</p>
<p>In May 1949, <a href="/wiki/Cleveland_Indians" title="Cleveland Indians">Cleveland Indians</a> outfielder <a href="/wiki/Larry_Doby" title="Larry Doby">Larry Doby</a> smacked the then-longest home run ever hit at the stadium over the right-center field wall, and onto a rooftop well outside the ballpark. The shot was reported to have traveled over 500 feet (150 m), and Doby called it "the longest homer I've ever hit".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 17, 1953, Mantle hit one off <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Stobbs" title="Chuck Stobbs">Chuck Stobbs</a> that was so impressive that someone tried to determine its flight with some precision, thus popularizing the term "Tape Measure Home Run". It was alleged to be 565 feet (172 m), although it bounced off the top of the back wall of the bleachers, adding some distance to its flight path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Clark Griffith once said that Josh Gibson hit more home runs into Griffith Stadium's distant left-field bleachers than the entire <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowry_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lowry-1">[1]</a></sup> Aside from some championship seasons in the early 1920s and 1930s, the Senators teams that played at Griffith Stadium were legendarily bad. The hapless Washington team became the butt of a well-known Vaudeville joke, "First in war, first in peace, and last in the American League", a twist on the famous <a href="/wiki/Henry_Lee_III" title="Henry Lee III">Henry "Light Horse Harry" Lee</a> eulogy of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>: "First in war, first in peace, and first in the hearts of his countrymen." (A similar phrasing was once used for the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Orioles#St._Louis_Browns" title="Baltimore Orioles">St. Louis Browns</a>: "First in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brown_Shoe_Company" title="Brown Shoe Company">shoes</a>, first in <a href="/wiki/Anheuser-Busch" title="Anheuser-Busch">booze</a>, and last in the American League.")</p>
<p>Only one Washington, D.C., public high school baseball player ever hit a home run over the 30-foot-high "green monster–like" right field wall at Griffith Stadium—Bill Harrison of Coolidge High School in 1952.</p>
<p>In 1915, Joseph P. Derby hit a home run off the right field wall, and became the only know amateur baseball player believed to hit a ball completely out of Washington's Griffith Stadium. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The stadium was still called Griffith Stadium in 1961, even though team owner <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Griffith" title="Calvin Griffith">Calvin Griffith</a> had moved the original Senators club to the Twin Cities area of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minneapolis-St._Paul" title="Minneapolis-St. Paul">Minneapolis-St. Paul</a> (becoming the <a href="/wiki/Minnesota_Twins" title="Minnesota Twins">Minnesota Twins</a>), to be replaced in Washington by a new <a href="/wiki/Expansion_team" title="Expansion team">expansion team</a>, also called the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)#Washington_Senators" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Senators</a> (now the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Texas Rangers</a>).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Football">Football</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Griffith_Stadium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Football">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Georgetown_vs_Marines.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="649" data-file-width="880" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Georgetown_vs_Marines.jpg/220px-Georgetown_vs_Marines.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Georgetown_vs_Marines.jpg/330px-Georgetown_vs_Marines.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Georgetown_vs_Marines.jpg/440px-Georgetown_vs_Marines.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Georgetown_vs_Marines.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Georgetown_Hoyas" title="Georgetown Hoyas">Georgetown Hoyas</a> versus the <a href="/wiki/Marine_Corps_Base_Quantico" title="Marine Corps Base Quantico">Quantico Marines</a> at Griffith Stadium, 1923</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Griffith Stadium hosted numerous football events before the Redskins moved there in 1937.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leavengood_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-Leavengood-6">[6]</a></sup> It was the home of <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_Hoyas_football" title="Georgetown Hoyas football">Georgetown Hoyas football</a> from 1921 until 1950, <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Colonials_football" title="George Washington Colonials football">George Washington Colonials football</a> in the 1930s and 1940s, and <a href="/wiki/Maryland_Terrapins_football" title="Maryland Terrapins football">Maryland Terrapins football</a> in 1948.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan-13">[13]</a></sup> The stadium was the host of an annual Thanksgiving Day game between <a href="/wiki/Howard_University" title="Howard University">Howard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_University_(Pennsylvania)" title="Lincoln University (Pennsylvania)">Lincoln</a> Universities, which was one of the most popular events during the year, drawing many African American alumni and fans from the surrounding neighborhoods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leavengood_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-Leavengood-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> One major reason for the stadium's early-1920s expansion was that Clark Griffith had envisioned the stadium hosting the annual Army–Navy football game, played every December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leavengood_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-Leavengood-6">[6]</a></sup> Temporary seats were often placed in right field for football games at Griffith, with the gridiron stretching from left field to the first base line. The exceptional distance from home to the left-field area allowed plenty of room for the football field.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Washington_Redskins">Washington Redskins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Griffith_Stadium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Washington Redskins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Redskins, previously based in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> and named for the Boston Braves, moved their home to D.C. in time for the <a href="/wiki/1937_Washington_Redskins_season" title="1937 Washington Redskins season">1937</a> season. As Boston, they had won their division in <a href="/wiki/1936_Boston_Redskins_season" title="1936 Boston Redskins season">1936</a> and continued their winning ways in Washington, capturing their first NFL championship in that first season. They continued as perennial contenders all through the war years.</p>
<p>Griffith Stadium was the location of <a href="/wiki/1940_NFL_Championship_Game" title="1940 NFL Championship Game">1940</a> and <a href="/wiki/1942_NFL_Championship_Game" title="1942 NFL Championship Game">1942</a> NFL Championship Games. The 1940 game was the 73–0 triumph by the <a href="/wiki/1940_Chicago_Bears_season" title="1940 Chicago Bears season">Chicago Bears</a> over the Redskins, the highest-scoring shutout game in the history of the NFL. The Championship Game in 1942 was essentially a rematch, with nearly the same players, and this time the Redskins upset the previously undefeated Bears, 14–6. According to Richard Whittingham's history of the Chicago Bears (<a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0671628852">ISBN 0671628852</a>), <a href="/wiki/George_Preston_Marshall" title="George Preston Marshall">George Preston Marshall</a>, the owner of the Redskins, gave his team a pre-game pep talk that consisted simply of writing "73–0" on the chalkboard.</p>
<p>During the Redskins' game on the afternoon of December 7, 1941, against the <a href="/wiki/1941_Philadelphia_Eagles_season" title="1941 Philadelphia Eagles season">Philadelphia Eagles</a>, an announcement was made over Griffith Stadium's public-address system commanding all of the American generals and admirals there to report to their duty stations. The <a href="/wiki/Attack_on_Pearl_Harbor" title="Attack on Pearl Harbor">Japanese attack on Pearl Harbor</a> was not explicitly announced over the P.A. system; with no small, portable radios available in the 1940s, that left thousands of other spectators to be among the last Americans to learn of the Japanese attack. The <a href="/wiki/1941_Washington_Redskins_season" title="1941 Washington Redskins season">Redskins</a> won that game, their last game of the <a href="/wiki/1941_NFL_season" title="1941 NFL season">1941 season</a>, 20-14. They finished the season with a record of six wins and five losses, in third place in the NFL Eastern Division. Although the Redskins enjoyed only one winning record at home (4–2 in 1956) during their final 13 seasons at Griffith, they still finished with an 81-58-6 overall record at the stadium over 24 seasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elfin_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elfin-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_events">Other events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Griffith_Stadium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Griffith Stadium was home to many events other than baseball and football. The <a href="/wiki/All-American_Girls_Professional_Baseball_League" title="All-American Girls Professional Baseball League">All-American Girls Professional Baseball League</a> played games there during the league's existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-19">[19]</a></sup> Over 180 boxing matches were held at the ballpark, including fights by <a href="/wiki/Joe_Louis" title="Joe Louis">Joe Louis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> On July 23, 1942, a "battle of music" was held at Griffith between musicians <a href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong" title="Louis Armstrong">Louis Armstrong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Barnet" title="Charlie Barnet">Charlie Barnet</a>. Some of the 18,000 fans in attendance began to dance on the field in front of the bandstand. Spectators who remained in their seats complained that they could not see. When the police attempted to control the situation by stopping the music, a riot broke out, with some minor injuries and several teenagers being hauled away by the police.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Scrap metal salvage rallies were held at least once at the stadium during World War II, where people would bring scrap metal to the ballpark to be melted and made into steel for new weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Griffith was also the home to public school events, church revivals, public meetings, and annual <a href="/wiki/Reserve_Officers%27_Training_Corps" title="Reserve Officers' Training Corps">ROTC</a> drill competitions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leavengood_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-Leavengood-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richards-23">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Billy_Graham" title="Billy Graham">Billy Graham</a> held a crusade at Griffith in 1960, preaching from a platform erected near second base. Two choir stands, each accommodating 500 singers, were set up along the foul lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Library_of_Congress_-_Ruth_knocked_out_(LOC).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3726" data-file-width="4615" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/The_Library_of_Congress_-_Ruth_knocked_out_%28LOC%29.jpg/260px-The_Library_of_Congress_-_Ruth_knocked_out_%28LOC%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/The_Library_of_Congress_-_Ruth_knocked_out_%28LOC%29.jpg/390px-The_Library_of_Congress_-_Ruth_knocked_out_%28LOC%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/The_Library_of_Congress_-_Ruth_knocked_out_%28LOC%29.jpg/520px-The_Library_of_Congress_-_Ruth_knocked_out_%28LOC%29.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Library_of_Congress_-_Ruth_knocked_out_(LOC).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Babe_Ruth" title="Babe Ruth">Babe Ruth</a> after being knocked unconscious during a 1924 game at Griffith; black fans are visible in the right field grandstand.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_in_racial_disputes">Status in racial disputes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Griffith_Stadium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Status in racial disputes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Griffith Stadium was located in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LeDroit_Park,_Washington,_D.C." title="LeDroit Park, Washington, D.C.">Ledroit Park Neighborhood</a>, of Washington, D.C., a historically black area since the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>. The neighborhood was home to many black working-class people, but also a class of young professional African American "elite" including <a href="/wiki/Langston_Hughes" title="Langston Hughes">Langston Hughes</a>. <a href="/wiki/Duke_Ellington" title="Duke Ellington">Duke Ellington</a> worked at Griffith selling hot dogs during his childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leavengood_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-Leavengood-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Griffith Stadium was not officially segregated, although an unofficial policy early after the 1920s expansion was that blacks sat in the right field pavilion. <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Griffith" title="Calvin Griffith">Calvin Griffith</a>, nephew of Clark Griffith, claimed that the segregated seats were a result of "colored preachers ... asking Mr. Griffith to put aside a section for the black people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leavengood_6-10"><a href="#cite_note-Leavengood-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Shortly after the end of World War I, after a report that several white women had been raped by a black man, a large group of whites seeking revenge marched toward the Shaw neighborhood. However, a conflict was avoided after these men came upon "a group of two thousand armed black men", who had been prepared and gotten their weapons at Griffith Stadium, chosen as a meeting place because of its landmark status in the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leavengood_6-11"><a href="#cite_note-Leavengood-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Senators management, seemingly uneasy about racial matters, were latecomers to integrating their team, hiring their first black player in late summer of 1954. The segregationist policy of the Redskins was more overt and controversial. In October 1957, a group of blacks picketed multiple times in front of Griffith Stadium, protesting the lack of black players on the Redskins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> It would be five more years before the Redskins finally began employing black players, the last NFL team to erase the color line.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Final_years">Final years</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Griffith_Stadium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Final years">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Howard_University_Hospital.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2147" data-file-width="2609" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Howard_University_Hospital.jpg/200px-Howard_University_Hospital.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Howard_University_Hospital.jpg/300px-Howard_University_Hospital.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Howard_University_Hospital.jpg/400px-Howard_University_Hospital.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Howard_University_Hospital.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Howard_University_Hospital" title="Howard University Hospital">Howard University Hospital</a><br/>
now occupies the stadium site</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1955, longtime Senators owner Clark Griffith died, and his nephew, <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Griffith" title="Calvin Griffith">Calvin Griffith</a>, took over. Fewer and fewer fans were coming to the ballpark, due in large part to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis_Browns" title="St. Louis Browns">St. Louis Browns</a>' move to Baltimore in 1954, causing Baltimoreans to stop having to come to Washington to see games.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jordan_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jordan-13">[13]</a></sup> Because of this, Calvin Griffith developed an interest in moving the Senators to <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>. Attendance may not have been the sole reason Griffith wanted to move, however; in a speech to Minnesota businessmen in the 1970s, Griffith said, "You only have 15,000 blacks here".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Griffith_plate.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="648" height="120" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Griffith_plate.jpg/180px-Griffith_plate.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Griffith_plate.jpg/270px-Griffith_plate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Griffith_plate.jpg/360px-Griffith_plate.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Griffith_plate.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Marker indicating the location of Griffith Stadium's home plate, inside <a href="/wiki/Howard_University_Hospital" title="Howard University Hospital">Howard University Hospital</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The American League opposed a move at first, but agreed under the condition that an expansion team, also named <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">the Senators</a>, would come to Washington, beginning in 1961.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The original Senators moved to Minnesota, and the new Senators played at Griffith in 1961. However, in 1962, the Senators moved to the new <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy_Memorial_Stadium" title="Robert F. Kennedy Memorial Stadium">D.C. Stadium</a>, joining the Redskins, who had moved there a year before. The final baseball game at Griffith Stadium was played on September 21, 1961, before a crowd of only 1,498 fans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Griffith Stadium now had no tenants, and sat empty for several years, deteriorating, with the field taking on the appearance of a prairie. The ballpark was demolished in 1965. Nearly a thousand of the stadium's seats were moved to <a href="/wiki/Tinker_Field" title="Tinker Field">Tinker Field</a> in <a href="/wiki/Orlando,_Florida" title="Orlando, Florida">Orlando, Florida</a>, at that time the spring training home of the Senators/Twins, where they remain today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BallparkTour.com_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-BallparkTour.com-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Howard_University_Hospital" title="Howard University Hospital">Howard University Hospital</a> now occupies the site. A marker has been placed in the building designating the location of home plate.</p>
<p>The expansion Senators remained in Washington through the <a href="/wiki/1971_Washington_Senators_season" title="1971 Washington Senators season">1971</a> season, then relocated to the <a href="/wiki/Dallas%E2%80%93Fort_Worth_metroplex" title="Dallas–Fort Worth metroplex">Dallas–Fort Worth metroplex</a> and became the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Rangers_(baseball)" title="Texas Rangers (baseball)">Texas Rangers</a> in <a href="/wiki/1972_Texas_Rangers_season" title="1972 Texas Rangers season">1972</a>.</p>

<li id="cite_note-8"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-8">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The seating capacity for baseball fluctuated over the years. According to <a class="external text" href="http://www.baseball-almanac.com/stadium/st_griff.shtml" rel="nofollow">Baseball Almanac</a>, capacities were as follows: 27,000 (1911–1920); 32,000 (1921–1935); 30,171 (1936–1938); 31,500 (1939); 29,473 (1940); 29,613 (1941–1946); 29,000 (1947); 25,048 (1948–1951); 35,000 (1952–1959); 28,669 (1960); 27,550 (1961). The seating capacity for football from 1937 through 1960 was 35,000.<a class="external autonumber" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=ZDjlaWRn2GIC&amp;q=%22griffith+stadium%22+almanac+redskins&amp;dq=%22griffith+stadium%22+almanac+redskins&amp;hl=en&amp;ei=YEGJTtDoNom2sQLMrdiGDw&amp;sa=X&amp;oi=book_result&amp;ct=result&amp;resnum=3&amp;ved=0CFkQ6AEwAg" rel="nofollow">[1]</a></span></li>

<div id="Washington_Redskins" style="font-size:114%"><a href="/wiki/Washington_Redskins" title="Washington Redskins"><span style="color:white">Washington Redskins</span></a></div>
</th>
</tr>
<tr style="height:2px">
<td colspan="2"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2" style="background: #FFB612; color:#660000;;">
<div>
<ul>
<li>Founded in 1932</li>
<li>Formerly the <b>Boston Braves</b> (1932) and <b>Boston Redskins</b> (1933–36)</li>
<li>Based in <b><a href="/wiki/Landover,_Maryland" title="Landover, Maryland"><span style="color:#660000">Landover, Maryland</span></a></b></li>
<li>Headquartered in <b><a href="/wiki/Ashburn,_Virginia" title="Ashburn, Virginia"><span style="color:#660000">Ashburn, Virginia</span></a></b></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
